Title: CITE: Anytime-Valid Statistical Inference in LLM Self-Consistency

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.05873v1

Published Time: Fri, 08 May 2026 00:58:09 GMT

Number of Pages: 71

Markdown Content:
CITE: ANYTIME-VALID STATISTICAL INFERENCE IN LLM SELF-CONSISTENCY 

HIROFUMI OTA, NAOTO IWASE, YUKI ICHIHARA, JUNPEI KOMIYAMA, AND MASAAKI IMAIZUMI Abstract . Large language models often improve reasoning by sampling multiple outputs and aggre-gating their final answers, but precise and e ffi cient control of error levels remains a challenging task. In particular, deciding when to stop sampling remains di ffi cult when the stopping rule is data-dependent and the set of possible response labels is not known in advance. We study anytime-valid certification of a prespecified target answer as the unique mode of the model’s response distribution, a guarantee distinct from answer correctness. We propose the Certification by Intersection-union Testing with E-processes (CITE) algorithm, which provably controls false certification at any prescribed level under arbitrary data-driven stopping, without requiring prior knowledge of the answer category set. We also prove a category-set-size-free stopping-time rate, establish matching minimax lower bounds up to constants in the main regime, and extend the construction to confidence-weighted voting. Simulations and LLM self-consistency experiments show empirical error control and improved certification in di ff use-tail settings. 

1. I ntroduction 

1.1. Background and Motivation. Large language models (LLMs) have demonstrated increasingly strong performance on a wide range of tasks Brown et al. (2020); Chowdhery et al. (2023); Achiam et al. (2023); Touvron et al. (2023), especially from the use of test-time compute . Prompting methods such as chain-of-thought and least-to-most decomposition encourage models to expose intermediate reasoning steps, while inference-time strategies such as self-consistency and tree-of-thought style search improve robustness by sampling, exploring, and aggregating multiple candidate reasoning trajectories (Wei et al., 2022; Zhou et al., 2023; Wang et al., 2023; Yao et al., 2023; Brown et al., 2024; Snell et al., 2025). A notion of adaptive self-consistency has gained attention to reduce computational cost of sampling growing approximately linearly with the number of sampled trajectories. A seminal approach is majority voting (Wang et al., 2023), which is simple, model-agnostic, and does not require a separate verifier, reward model, or additional training. In particular, the method adapts to the case that easy questions often stabilize after only a few samples, whereas hard questions remain ambiguous and may benefit from continued sampling. As extensions, Aggarwal et al. (2023) studies a stopping method based on posterior agreement among sampled answers; Li et al. (2024) uses low-entropy windows as a practical stopping signal; Wang et al. (2025b) and Wang et al. 

Date : May 8, 2026 (first version). 

Key words and phrases. test-time compute, e-processes, sequential mode testing, unseen categories. 

> 1
> arXiv:2605.05873v1 [stat.ML] 7 May 2026

Figure 1. Overview of the stopping rule by CITE. With the countably infinite set of categories A, CITE tests the hypothesis of whether the target category r ∈ A is a mode or not. 

(2025a) allocate budgets using di ffi culty estimates or bandit-style uncertainty across sets of queries; and confidence-aware variants use response-level confidence to improve aggregation and stopping Taubenfeld et al. (2025); Aghazadeh et al. (2025). One challenge of the literature is to handle the situation where we must precisely control error level ε of certifying a modal response, those that arise when the risk of failure is high. A typical real example is the emergency-room triage on data such as ER-REASON (Mehandru et al., 2025), where each LLM-output acuity score determines the time-to-physician for an arriving patient and each disposition determines admission-versus-discharge. In this case, if a modal-response certificate is used operationally, the false-certification probability must be bounded at a prescribed level ε. At present, it is di ffi cult to control the certification errors with precision; therefore, keeping errors below a certain threshold requires performing an excessive number of inferences, which directly leads to a massive increase in computational costs. Based on the situation, we have the following question: can we derive a data-driven stopping rule for adaptive self-consistency that controls the error level of certifying a modal response by LLMs? 

1.2. Our Study. In this study, we propose the Certification by Intersection-union Testing with E-processes (CITE), which is an anytime-valid and category-agnostic sequential test for controlling the Type-I error of falsely certifying a prespecified response label as the unique mode. We note the important features: (i) Anytime validity : CITE works with data-driven stopping time and the error guarantee must hold uniformly over the time, not at a single prespecified sample size. (ii) 

Category-agnostic , CITE works even when the response category is unknown a priori and grows as sampling proceeds, which can adapt to new responses from LLMs. CITE gives a stopping time based on the three conditions on empirical probabilities: (i) the target is larger than the runner-up, (ii) the target itself is large, and (iii) the unobserved category is small, using anytime-valid e-process 

> 2

machinery (Ville, 1939; Grünwald et al., 2024; Ramdas and Wang, 2025). Figure 1 gives a graphical abstract. Our contributions are as follows. 

• Anytime-valid category-agnostic error guarantee : CITE controls Type-I error of certify-ing a label as the unique mode uniformly over all stopping times at any prescribed level ε

(Theorem 4.1). The error guarantee is valid without the knowledge of answer categories. 

• Category-size-free stopping-time : We show that the stopping time by CITE has an order which is free from category size (Theorem 4.3), which is contrast to several baseline methods. We also derive a matching minimax lower bound (Theorem 4.4). 

• Extension to weighted case : We extend CITE to handle a case with weighted observations and gaps. We also derive an anytime-valid Type-I control and yield the same rate of the stopping time (Section 5). 

• Empirical performance : Simulations and LLM experiments (Section 6) confirm that CITE controls Type-I error empirically, matches or improves on baselines on three LLM benchmarks. 2. P roblem Formulation 

2.1. Setup and Observation. Let A be a countable (possibly infinite) set of response categories. We consider an unknown categorical distribution P on A, and write pa := P(X = a) for its probability mass function for a ∈ A . Let p(1) ≥ p(2) ≥ · · · denote the decreasing rearrangement of (pa)a∈A , and let Ft := σ(X1, . . . , Xt) be the natural filtration. Suppose that we observe an i.i.d. sequence X1, X2, . . . from P:

Assumption 2.1 (i.i.d. categorical data) . (Xt)t≥1 are i.i.d. draws from a categorical distribution P on a countable set A.

We define some notations for statistics from the observations. For each a ∈ A and t ≥ 1, let 

Nt(a) : =

> t

X

> i=1

1{Xi = a}, bpt(a) : = Nt(a)

t , At := {a ∈ A : Nt(a) ≥ 1}

denote the count, empirical frequency, and set of observed categories by time t.Our definition of P has a distinctive feature: A may contain a countably infinite number of categories, and since A is unknown, information about the set of categories cannot be used in decision-making. This setup is e ff ective for representing the complex response patterns of LLMs. 2.2. Statistical Testing. We study the fixed-target unique-mode testing problem. For a target category r ∈ A fixed a priori , i.e. independent of the data, we design a sequential test of the unique-mode hypothesis that r strictly dominates every other category. 

> 3

Definition 2.2 (Null and alternative hypothesis) . For a fixed target category r ∈ A , set H0,r := P : pr ≤ sup 

> a,r

pa , H1,r := P : pr > sup 

> a,r

pa .

H1,r is the unique-mode alternative, i.e. r strictly dominates every other category. Under any P ∈ H0,r there exists a deterministic witness a⋆ , r such that pa⋆ ≥ pr holds. A procedure certifies rwhenever it rejects H 0,r in favor of H 1,r.

To handle this testing, we consider certifying the mode through a sequential procedure with the observations using the following criteria. 

Definition 2.3 (Sequential certification procedure) . A sequential certification procedure for target ris a sequence (Dt)t≥1 of Ft-measurable decision rules, where Dt = 1 means that, after observing X1, . . . , Xt, the procedure certifies r as the unique mode. Its stopping time is 

τ := inf {t ≥ 1 : Dt = 1},

with inf ∅ = ∞. The procedure has anytime-valid Type-I error control at level ε if it holds that 

sup 

> P∈H0,r

PP(τ < ∞) ≤ ε. (1) 

If τ < ∞, the procedure certifies r as the unique mode. 

Importantly, the sampling time t is not fixed in advance, hence The bound (1) is time-uniform. At each time t, the procedure either continues sampling or stops and certifies, using only the data observed so far. The Type-I guarantee controls the probability that such a data-dependent rule ever falsely certifies r.

Remark 2.4. We note the originality of our setting. While similar settings have been considered in statistics (Good, 1953; Painsky, 2025) and LLM test-time compute (Aggarwal et al., 2023; Li et al., 2024; Komiyama et al., 2026), to the best of our knowledge, an anytime valid sequential testing for the infinite and unknown category set has not been investigated. This setting describes with the nature of LLMs and represents a novel approach in terms of hypothesis testing. 

3. CITE: C ertification by Intersection -union Testing with E-processes 

We present our proposed method, CITE. Here, the target r ∈ A and the error level of certification 

ε > 0 is fixed before observing data as formalized in Kim and Ramdas (2025). Our strategy involves using three data-driven stochastic processes to estimate the components of the distribution uniformly over time. In particular, we consider Ft-measurable stochastic processes Et, Lt and Ut with their forms will be derived in the following sections, which satisfies the following conditions under the null hypothesis H0,r:(1) Pairwise evaluation : For a ∈ A , Et evaluates an relative size of pr against pa, that is, for any α ∈ (0 , 1), sup t≥1 Et ≤ 1/α holds with probability at least 1 − α.

> 4

(2) Lower bound of the target probability pr: the second process Lt bounds pr below, that is, sup t≥1 Lt ≤ pr holds. (3) Upper bound of unseen categories : the third process Ut is an upper bound of probability of unseen categories, that is, inf t≥1 Ut ≥ sup a∈A :Nt(a)=0 pa holds. Note that these stochastic processes are calculated from observed values X1, ..., Xt without the knowledge of P.Using these processes, we define our stopping rule: if any of the conditions above satisfied by these stochastic processes is violated at time t, we reject the null hypothesis H0,r and stop as τ = t.Rigorously, the stopping rule, with P ∈ H0,r and a⋆ , r satisfying pa⋆ ≥ pr, provides the following inclusion: 

{τ < ∞} ⊆ ∃t : Et ≥ 3/ε

We develop a time-uniform lower confidence bound (LCB) on the target probability pr to handle hidden competitors ,i.e., categories h ∈ A with ph > pr that have never appeared in the sample. For each candidate value q ∈ (0 , 1], we test a hypothesis “ pr ≤ q” by betting on the centered indicator 1{Xi = r} − q, which is an analogous of 1{Xi = r} − 1{Xi = a} in Component (i). 3.3. Component (iii): Unseen Upper Bound. We develop an upper bound for the probability of any currently unseen category, simultaneously over all categories and times. A category of mass 

u ∈ (0 , 1) remains unseen after t i.i.d. draws with probability (1 − u)t, and a probability-weighted 

union bound turns this fact into a time-uniform per-category upper bound, bypassing the need to estimate the aggregate missing mass. 

Definition 3.5 (Unseen upper bound) . An upper bound of the unseen probabilities is defined as Ut := min u ∈ (0 , 1] : u−1(1 − u)t ≤ ε/ 3 .

The threshold balances two forces: (1 − u)t decays exponentially in u, while the u−1 factor pays the union-bound cost over rare categories. As a result, we obtain the following guarantee: 

Proposition 3.6 (Simultaneous validity of the unseen bound) . Under Assumption 2.1, we obtain 

P ∃a ∈ A , ∃t ≥ 1 : Nt(a) = 0 and p a > Ut

 ≤ ε/ 3.

3.4. Stopping Rule. We define the CITE stopping rule by combining these three stochastic pro-cesses above, which are uniform in time. In particular, we design the stopping rule to follow the inclusion of the events as (2). Algorithm 1 shows the pseudo-code of the CITE stopping rule. 

> 6

Definition 3.7 (CITE stopping rule) . With given ε > 0, CITE stops at the first time both (a) Pairwise rejection: Et ≥ 3/ε at the empirical runner-up, and (b) LCB exceeds unseen bound: Lt > Ut, hold simultaneously, i.e. we define the stopping time as 

τ := inf t ≥ 1 : Et ≥ 3/ε and L t > Ut . (6) 

If τ < ∞, the procedure certifies r as the unique mode.

We establish three theoretical guarantees for CITE. First, the false certification probability is controlled at the prescribed anytime-valid level for the fixed target r (Theorem 4.1). Second, under the unique-mode alternative, the expected stopping time is bounded by a category-set-size-free rate depending on ( δ, pr) but not on |A| (Theorem 4.3). Third, a combined information-theoretic lower bound shows that this rate is optimal up to constants and logarithmic terms (Theorem 4.4). 4.1. Main Theorems. We first provide the sequential version of Type-I error control on the stopping time τ given in Definition 3.7. 7Theorem 4.1 (Type-I error control) . Let ε ∈ (0 , 1) be a prespecified significance level. Under Assumption 2.1, the stopping time τ of Definition 3.7 satisfies 

sup 

> P∈H0,r

PP(τ < ∞) ≤ ε. 

The proof, deferred to Appendix B.6, fixes a null witness a⋆ , r satisfying pa⋆ ≥ pr. If this witness has been observed by the stopping time, runner-up monotonicity implies that the fixed-competitor e-process E(r,a⋆) 

> t

must have crossed 3 /ε . If the witness is still unseen, then the inequality 

Lt > Ut can hold only if either the LCB or unseen bound has failed. This yields (2) and a union bound over the three ε/ 3 components. The above result on the Type-I error control does not provide any guarantee regarding the time required to certify the true mode. Under the alternative, write the modal gap as δ := pr − p(2) > 0. We now show that E[τ] is bounded by a function of ( δ, pr) alone, independent of |A| .

Condition 4.2. Λpw contains some λpw ∈ [δ/ 8, δ/ 4] , and Λr contains some λr ∈ [1 /32 , 1/16] .

Theorem 4.3 (Stopping-time bound) . Suppose Assumption 2.1 holds and Condition 4.2 with δ > 0,and the selected grid weights wλpw , vλr are bounded below by a constant independent of δ, pr, ε .Then, we have P(τ < ∞) = 1 and 

E[τ] = O log(1 /ε ) + log(1 /δ )

δ2 + log(1 /ε ) + log(1 /pr)

with universal constants independent of δ, p r, |A| .

δ ∈ (0 , p), define 

P(p, δ ) : = P : pr = p, p(2) ≤ p − δ .

Theorem 4.4 (Combined minimax lower bound) . Fix p ∈ (0 , 1/2] and δ ∈ (0 , p). Assume either that A is countably infinite, or that it contains at least ⌈(1 − p)/(p − δ)⌉ + 2 distinct non-target categories. Let τ′ be any level-ε sequential certification procedure for the fixed target r , i.e., 

sup Q∈H0,r PQ(τ′ < ∞) ≤ ε. Assume that, for every P ∈ P (p, δ ), PP(τ′ < ∞) = 1 and EP[τ′] < ∞ hold. Then, there is a universal constant c > 0 such that 

sup

Comparing the lower bound of Theorem 4.4 with the upper bound (7) , we show that the CITE stopping time achieves the minimax optimal order up to logarithmic factors whenever pr is bounded away from zero. In the very small-pr regime, the current upper bound can be worse in the pairwise term; closing this gap is left open. In Appendix F, we give extensions of the analysis: sharper bounds with refined constant (Theo-rem F.2), a growth rate of the e-process (Theorem F.8); a stopping time bound with an geometric 

> 8

grid (Theorem F.5); and a top-k extension for the Type-I error and the stopping-time bound (Theo-rem F.9). 5. E xtension to Confidence -W eighted Voting 

5.1. Setup and Weighted CITE. We extend CITE to the confidence-weighted case: suppose we observe a pair ( Xi, Wi) with a confidence score Wi ∈ [0 , 1], e.g. the score from logits or a verifier; see (Taubenfeld et al., 2025; Fu et al., 2025). In this case, our purpose is to certify the weighted unique mode arg max a μa with μa := E[Wi1{Xi = a}] and weighted modal gap ∆w := μr − μ⋆, where 

μ⋆ := sup a,r μa.

Assumption 5.1 (i.i.d. weighted categories) . (Xi, Wi)i≥1 are i.i.d. with X i ∈ A and W i ∈ [0 , 1] .

without reducing the target weighted mass μr too much. 6. E mpirical Validation 

We evaluate CITE on simulated multinomial distributions and on LLM self-consistency bench-marks. The experiments are designed to evaluate the following quantities: empirical false certifica-tion (Type-I error) under null targets, empirical power measured by certification probability under alternatives, and stopping time or sample e ffi ciency, both on synthetic multinomial models and on LLM answer distributions. Baselines are Bonferroni (fixed-sample union bound), KR (Kim and Ramdas, 2025) (fixed-target sample-split test), and MMC (Cordero-Encinar and Duncan, 2025) (sequential leader-tracking), all at level ε = 0.05. We do not include adaptive stopping methods without theoretical guarantees of Type-I error control. Detailed protocols, per-setting tables, and ablations on component scaling and the confidence-weighted W-CITE are deferred to Appendices G and H. Simulations. We evaluate CITE on five multinomial settings (Appendix G.1). First, we check false certification under the fixed-target null. In Case B, across all five settings and all sample sizes, CITE has empirical false-certification rate zero, consistent with Theorem 4.1; the same is true for W-CITE and fixed-sample Bonferroni. By contrast, KR shows small nonzero false-certification rates on Settings 3–4, up to 0 .002, and MMC shows nonzero false certifications in the same regimes, reaching 0 .006 on Setting 3 and 0 .014 on Setting 4 (Table 3). Second, we evaluate certification power and stopping time under the alternative. In the easy concentrated Setting 2, all methods saturate quickly: at N = 64, CITE certifies with rate 0 .890 and mean stopping time ¯τ ≈ 36, while Bonferroni and KR achieve rates 0 .928 and 0 .946, respectively. In the di ff use Setting 1, CITE is more e ffi cient than Bonferroni before saturation, with certification rates 0 .188 vs. 0 .100 at N = 64 and 0 .892 vs. 0 .804 at N = 128, reaching full certification by 

> 10

N = 256 with ¯τ ≈ 90. In the moderate LLM-like Setting 5, CITE reaches rate 0 .544 at N = 256 and 0.932 at N = 512 with ¯τ ≈ 234, whereas MMC remains at rate at most 0 .002 through N = 2048. KR often has higher finite-sample power, but it is a fixed-sample baseline rather than an anytime-valid stopping rule. In the near-tie Setting 3 and very di ff use Setting 4, all methods remain low-power within the evaluated budget range. Finally, the component-level simulations are consistent with the two terms in Theorem 4.3. When 

pr is varied at fixed gap, the LCB–unseen component follows the predicted p−1 

> r

dependence; in the reported sweep, ¯τlu pr stays between roughly 14 and 20. When δ is varied at fixed pr = 0.24, the LCB–unseen component stays approximately constant, around ¯τlu ≈ 70, while the pairwise component is the part most a ff ected by the modal gap (Appendix G.3). LLM self-consistency. We evaluate the performance of Qwen3-30B ( Qwen3-30B-A3B-Instruct-2507 ) (Qwen Team, 2025) and gpt-oss-20b ( openai/gpt-oss-20b ) (OpenAI, 2025) on AIME 2026 (Dekon-inck et al., 2026), FrontierScience-Olympiad (Wang et al., 2026), and the clinical benchmark ER-REASON (Mehandru et al., 2025). Figure 2 plots mean accuracy against the sample budget; the horizontal axis is the mean stopping time for the sequential methods (CITE, W-CITE, MMC) and the prefix sample size N for the fixed-sample baselines (Bonferroni, KR). When the target is set to the empirical runner-up rather than the mode, the maximum false-certification rate over all evaluated methods, problems, and budgets is 0 .052 on gpt-oss-20b /ER-REASON and at most 0 .012 on every other tested (model, dataset) pair; both lie within Monte Carlo error of the nominal level ε = 0.05 given the multiplicity of comparisons. On Qwen3-30B /ER-REASON, fixed-sample Bonferroni saturates at certification rate 0 .111 for N ≥ 256, whereas CITE attains certification rate ≥ 0.98 at mean stopping time ¯τ ≈ 12. This gives a direct stopping-time diagnostic: in this concentrated-answer regime, CITE certifies after roughly a dozen samples on average, while the fixed-sample baseline remains weak even at much larger budgets. At the largest evaluated budget N = 1024, the CITE certification rate exceeds that of MMC by a factor of approximately 2 on FrontierScience-Olympiad (where the empirical answer set contains about 250 distinct values per problem on gpt-oss-20b), while on ER-REASON the two methods di ff er by at most 0 .054 across all budgets, attained on gpt-oss-20b. Relative to the KR baseline, the CITE certification rate is lower by at most 0 .087, attained on Qwen3-30B /FrontierScience at 

N ∈ { 256 , 512 }, while retaining anytime validity, whereas KR, by construction, is not valid under adaptive stopping. Per-problem and per-budget tables and the corresponding results for the W-CITE are deferred to Appendix H. 7. C onclusion 

We introduced CITE for sequentially certifying the unique mode of a discrete distribution on a countable, possibly infinite category set. Our theory guarantees that anytime-valid Type-I error control, the stopping time has the category-set-size free order, and the order matches the information-theoretic lower bound up to logarithmic factors. In our experiments, both numerical simulations and real LLM environments demonstrated that CITE achieves high accuracy while controlling Type-I errors. Limitations and future work. CITE provides statistical guarantee that a prespecified label is the unique mode of the model’s response distribution. This is logically distinct from, and complementary to, answer correctness : the two compose, and neither subsumes the other, so a downstream system can combine our anytime-valid mode certificate with any separate correctness signal. Within this scope, a natural extension is to certify the empirically observed top category brt ∈ arg max a Nt(a), 

12 i.e., a data-adaptive target; this requires a Bonferroni-type correction over the random sequence of empirical leaders, paralleling the construction of Cordero-Encinar and Duncan (2025), and whether the category-size-free rate of Theorem 4.3 survives this correction is left open. References

Appendix A. R elated Works 

Self-consistency and test-time compute for LLM reasoning. Self-consistency and related inference-time scaling methods improve LLM reasoning by sampling multiple reasoning trajectories and aggregating their terminal answers, typically by majority voting (Wei et al., 2022; Zhou et al., 2023; Wang et al., 2023; Yao et al., 2023; Brown et al., 2024; Snell et al., 2025). These methods motivate our statistical formulation: after normalization, repeated LLM outputs are viewed as i.i.d. draws from an unknown categorical distribution over terminal response labels. Our work is not a new prompting, search, or reranking method. Instead, it studies when one may stop sampling and certify, with a prescribed error level, that a prespecified response label is the unique mode of this distribution. This is a guarantee about the model-induced response distribution, and is distinct from semantic correctness of the answer. Adaptive self-consistency and early stopping. A growing line of work reduces the cost of self-consistency by adapting the number of sampled trajectories to the apparent di ffi culty of each query. Adaptive-Consistency uses posterior agreement among sampled answers as a lightweight stopping signal (Aggarwal et al., 2023); Early-Stopping Self-Consistency stops when a low-entropy window indicates su ffi cient agreement (Li et al., 2024); di ffi culty-adaptive and dynamic variants allocate inference budgets using di ffi culty estimates, answer-distribution uncertainty, or reasoning-path quality (Wang et al., 2025b,a); and confidence-aware methods use response-level confidence or verifier-like scores to improve aggregation and stopping (Taubenfeld et al., 2025; Aghazadeh et al., 2025; Fu et al., 2025). These methods are primarily designed to improve the accuracy–cost tradeo ff .By contrast, CITE is designed for statistical certification: it controls the probability of falsely certifying the target as the unique mode under arbitrary data-driven stopping, at a user-specified level ε.Certified self-consistency. The closest LLM-specific statistical work is the Martingale Majority Certificate (MMC) of Cordero-Encinar and Duncan (2025), which develops finite-sample and anytime-valid certificates for self-consistency and uses martingale arguments to determine when enough samples have been drawn. MMC provides an important first step toward certified self-consistency, but it certifies a stronger majority /residual-tail condition than the fixed-target unique-mode property considered here. Our target di ff ers in two respects. First, CITE tests a fixed-target unique-mode alternative, pr > sup a,r pa, whereas MMC-style certificates are naturally tied to a stronger majority or residual-tail domination condition. These two notions coincide only in non-di ff use regimes. In LLM self-consistency, however, the modal answer may have substantially larger mass than every individual competitor while still being smaller than the aggregate mass of the long tail. Proposition E.1 shows that on a provable least-favourable family in this regime, the MMC certifies with probability at most the nominal level ε. Second, CITE explicitly handles the possibility that a high-mass competitor has not yet been observed. This unseen-category component is essential when the set of normalized free-form answers is unknown and grows during sampling. 

> 17

Anytime-valid inference, e-values, and confidence sequences. Our construction builds on the classical martingale view of sequential testing (Ville, 1939) and the modern theory of e-values, e-processes, testing by betting, and confidence sequences (Howard et al., 2020, 2021; Shafer, 2021; Vovk and Wang, 2021; Lindon and Malek, 2022; Grünwald et al., 2024; Ramdas and Wang, 2025). These tools provide tests and confidence statements that remain valid under continuous monitoring and data-dependent stopping. CITE instantiates this machinery for a composite categorical unique-mode problem: it combines pairwise e-processes against observed competitors with a time-uniform lower confidence bound for the target mass and an upper bound for unseen competitors. The resulting intersection-union test yields a certification rule whose Type-I error is valid uniformly over all stopping times. Best-arm identification and discrete argmin inference. The fixed-target unique-mode problem is related to fixed-confidence best-arm identification and pure-exploration bandits (Garivier and Kaufmann, 2016; Kaufmann et al., 2016; Kaufmann and Koolen, 2021). Those works characterize the sample complexity of identifying the best arm under sequential sampling and often rely on a known finite set of arms. Our setting is di ff erent: each LLM query returns a single categorical draw from the entire response distribution, the category set is not known in advance, and a previously unseen response can become a relevant competitor. The closest fixed-target finite-dimensional inference problem is the dimension-agnostic discrete argmin test of Kim and Ramdas (2025), which we include as a baseline. CITE di ff ers by providing an anytime-valid stopping rule for a countable and unknown category set, rather than a fixed-sample or sample-split test over a given finite vector. Unseen species, missing mass, and support-agnostic inference. The need to reason about unobserved response labels connects our work to the classical unseen-species and missing-mass literature, including Good–Turing and Good–Toulmin estimation (Good, 1953; Good and Toulmin, 1956), concentration bounds for the missing mass (McAllester and Ortiz, 2003; Berend and Kontorovich, 2013), and minimax prediction of unseen species (Orlitsky et al., 2016). These works estimate or bound quantities such as the aggregate probability of unseen outcomes or the number of new species. CITE uses a di ff erent object: for certification of a unique mode, it is enough to rule out the existence of a single unseen competitor whose mass could exceed the target. The unseen component of CITE is therefore tailored to sequential modal certification rather than to estimating the entire missing mass or support size. Confidence-weighted aggregation. Recent work shows that response-level confidence can improve self-consistency by weighting or prioritizing high-confidence reasoning paths (Taubenfeld et al., 2025; Aghazadeh et al., 2025; Fu et al., 2025). Our weighted extension formalizes a complementary question: when the observations are pairs ( Xi, Wi) with bounded confidence weights, can one certify that a target label is the unique weighted mode? W-CITE extends the same anytime-valid and category-agnostic guarantee to this weighted setting, with the weighted gap replacing the unweighted modal gap in the stopping-time analysis. 

> 18

Step 2 (bounding P(E2)). On E2, CITE stops at τ, so Lτ(r) > Uτ, and a⋆ is unseen at τ. Define the two failure events

Fu := {∃ a ∈ A , ∃t ≥ 1 : Nt(a) = 0 and pa > Ut}.

pa⋆ ≤ Uτ < Lτ(r) ≤ pr,

Lemma C.4 (Quadratic lower bound for log(1 + x)) . For every x ∈ [−1/2, 1/2] ,

log(1 + x) ≥ x − x2.

Proof. Let h(x) : = log (1 + x) − x + x2. Then h(0) = 0 and h′(x) = x(1 + 2x)/(1 + x), which is nonpositive on [ −1/2, 0] and nonnegative on [0 , 1/2]. Thus h(x) ≥ 0 on [ −1/2, 1/2]. □

Lemma C.10 (Stopped KL identity for the I-projection) . Under the assumptions of Lemma C.9, if

Proof of Theorem 4.4. Fix p ∈ (0 , 1/2], δ ∈ (0 , p), and a level-ε sequential certifier τ′ satisfying the assumptions of the corollary. For notational simplicity, write τ := τ′ within this proof, and let

D.4. Weighted LCB validity (Section 5, item (ii)). 

Proof. If μr = 0, then Wi1{Xi = r} = 0 almost surely for all i, so bμt(r) = 0 and eLt(r) = 0 almost surely. Hence {∃ t : eLt(r) > μ r} is empty. Assume μr > 0 in the remainder. The proof parallels that of Proposition 3.4 (Appendix B.4), with the following substitutions: 

1{Xi = r} becomes Wi1{Xi = r}, pr becomes μr, bpt(r) becomes bμt(r), and the mixture weights ( wλ)become ( vλ). At the true value q = μr, one has E[Wi1{Xi = r} − μr] = 0, so each eMt(μr, λ ) is a nonnegative martingale with unit initial value, and Ville’s inequality gives P( sup t eMt(μr) ≥ 1/α r) ≤

αr. The monotonicity from the weighted LCB monotonicity property then implies {∃ t : eLt(r) >μr} ⊆ { sup t eMt(μr) ≥ 1/α r}. □

Lemma D.3 (Weighted small-bet drift bounds) . Assume ∆w > 0.

(a) Pairwise: if λ ∈ Λpw satisfies λ ≤ ∆w/ 2( μr + μ⋆), then

(b) LCB: for any q 0 ∈ (0 , μ r) and any λ ∈ Λr with λ ≤ (μr − q0)/(2 μr), one has λ < 1/q0 and 

Elog  1 + λ(W1{X = r} − q0) ≥ λ(μr − q0)/2 > 0.

(c) Universal LCB grid point: with q0 := μr/2 in part (b), the condition reduces to λr ∈ (0 , 1/4] ,and

Proof. All three parts follow from the quadratic lower bound log (1 + ξ) ≥ ξ − ξ2 on [ −1/2, 1/2] (Lemma C.4), exactly as in Proposition C.5, with two additional ingredients: W ∈ [0 , 1] implies 

W2 ≤ W, and the squared indicators satisfy ( 1{X = r} − 1{X = a})2 = 1{X = r} + 1{X = a}.

(a) Set ξa := λW(1{X = r}− 1{X = a}), so |ξa| ≤ λ ≤ 1/2. Taking expectations of log (1 +ξa) ≥ ξa −ξ2

> a

and using W2 ≤ W,

E[log(1 + ξa)] ≥ λ(μr − μa) − λ2(μr + μa) ≥ λ∆w − λ2(μr + μ⋆).

The hypothesis gives λ2(μr + μ⋆) ≤ λ∆w/2, hence E[log(1 + ξa)] ≥ λ∆w/2 uniformly in a.

(b) Since μr ≤ 1, ( μr − q0)/(2 μr) < 1/2 < 1/q0, so the hypothesis implies λ < 1/2 and λ < 1/q0.Setting Y := W1{X = r} and ξ := λ(Y − q0), the same quadratic bound and Y2 ≤ Y yield 

E[log(1 + ξ)] ≥ λ(μr − q0) − λ2μr ≥ λ(μr − q0)/2.

> 34

(c) Specializing (b) to q0 = μr/2 gives ( μr − q0)/(2 μr) = 1/4, so λr ≤ 1/4 verifies the hypothesis and the conclusion follows. □

D.8. Proof of Lemma D.1. 

Proof. The proof follows the same symmetrization argument as Lemma C.2, applied to the bounded function class { fa(x, w) : = gλ(w)1{x = a} : a , r} in place of {1{x = a}} . The key modifications are: 

• The centering term tp (2) becomes tv ⋆λ , where v⋆λ := sup a,r E[gλ(W)1{X = a}]. 

• The per-coordinate bound ∥va∥∞ ≤ 1 becomes ∥va∥∞ ≤ gλ(1) = − log (1 − λ), so the sub-Gaussian parameter scales by − log(1 − λ). 

• The Rademacher complexity bound remains O( pt log( t + 1) ) because there are still at most 

t + 1 distinct vectors in the function class evaluated on t samples. Optimizing the Donsker–Varadhan parameter s as in Lemma C.2 yields the stated bound. □

D.9. Proof of Lemma D.2. 

Proof. (a) Assume bμt(r) ≤ q. Set Yi := Wi1{Xi = r}. By Jensen’s inequality (concavity of log ), 

t−1 log eMt(q, λ ) ≤ log (1 + λ(bμt(r) − q)) ≤ 0 for every λ ∈ Λr(q). Summing gives eMt(q) ≤ P vλ ≤ 1, contradicting eMt(q) > 1. 

(b) If eMt(q) ≥ 1/α r > 1, part (a) gives bμt(r) > q, so q ∈ (0 ,bμt(r)] and q belongs to the set defining 

eLt(r); hence eLt(r) ≥ q. □

Appendix E. C omparison with Martingale Majority Certificates 

E.1. The MMC Hypothesis. The top-m MMC (Cordero-Encinar and Duncan, 2025) simultane-ously tests the leader against m − 1 pairwise competitors and the aggregated residual mass. The alternative certified upon stopping is 

HMMC ,m := {P : p(1) > p(i)∀i = 2, . . . , m} ∩ { P : p(1) > sm}, sm :=

X

> j>m

p( j). (21) 

Proposition E.1 (Strict inclusion) . Suppose the target r coincides with the (unique) modal label pr = p(1) , equivalently r is the unique maximiser of a 7 → pa. Then for every m ≥ 2, HMMC ,m ⊊ H1,r;without the unique-mode-target identification, this strict inclusion must be read with H1,r replaced by Sr′∈A H1,r′ .

Remark E.2 (The di ff use-tail regime) . Distributions in H1,r \ H MMC ,m have a unique mode that does not dominate the aggregate tail: p(1) > p(2) but p(1) ≤ sm. Several of our LLM self-consistency settings (Section 6) fall into this regime, with the mode-answer carrying 20 –30% of the mass and thousands of alternative answers sharing the remainder. For each fixed m, the MMC controls its certification rate at level α on any such P : its residual-mass channel at that m tests the null 

{p(1) ≤ sm}, which is true under P , so the corresponding e-process cannot grow past the rejection threshold with probability > α . An MMC variant that tries multiple m in parallel pays a Bonferroni-style cost over the active tuples (Appendix E.3). 38 E.2. The Switching-Null Challenge. At each round n, the MMC selects the empirical leader An−1

and runner-up Bn−1 (both Fn−1-measurable), maintaining e-processes: erun

Appendix F. A dditional Theoretical Results 

This appendix collects additional results used to interpret and extend the main theory: a sharp oracle pairwise benchmark (F.1), an adaptive geometric grid (F.2), a growth-rate optimality result for the oracle pairwise bet and its finite mixture (F.3), and a top-k extension (F.4). F.1. Sharp oracle pairwise benchmark. The main text compares the stopping-time upper bound with the minimax lower bound at the level of rates. Here we isolate a sharper constant-level statement for an oracle pairwise comparison between the target and its true runner-up. This result is not a sharp-constant theorem for the full CITE stopping time; rather, it shows that the pairwise e-process has the correct leading constant when both the true runner-up and the oracle betting parameter are available. 

Proposition F.1 (Identity and small-gap expansion of ρ⋆). The oracle pairwise growth rate ρ⋆ :=

max λ∈(0 ,1) 

pr log (1 + λ) + p(2) log (1 − λ) (Proposition C.6) coincides exactly with the forward I-projection KL of P onto H 0,r:

ρ⋆ = D(P∥P⋆) = pr log 2pr

+ p(2) log 2p(2)

In particular, by Lemma C.9,

Remark F.3 (Scope of the oracle result) . Theorem F.2 is an oracle benchmark for comparing r with its true runner-up. It is not a sharp-constant theorem for the full CITE stopping time, which also uses the empirical runner-up, the LCB–unseen check, and a finite grid. 

F.2. Adaptive geometric grid. Condition 4.2 requires a grid point in [ δ/ 8, δ/ 4], which depends on the unknown gap δ. A geometric grid gives a uniform version of the same rate over all δ ≥ δ0.

Condition F.4 (Geometric grid) . For a fixed minimum gap δ0 ∈ (0 , 1] , set 

Λgeo pw := {2−k : 1 ≤ k ≤ K}, K := log 2(8 /δ 0) ,

with uniform weights w λ = 1/K.

Remark F.6. The geometric grid removes the need to know δ exactly, but it still requires a lower design scale δ0. Removing this design parameter would require a separate adaptive-betting analysis, which we do not pursue here.

Remark F.10. The top- k extension preserves the runner-up reduction within the “outsider” set, so the per-round cost is k pairwise checks and k LCB updates, plus one shared unseen bound. The case k = 1 recovers CITE of Definition 3.7, with Type-I and rate guarantees given by Theorems 4.1 and 4.3 respectively.

G.3. Bottleneck Analysis. 

G.4. Logistic Weight Model Saturation. As discussed in Section 6, the logistic weight model can saturate when K is large. For Settings 1 and 5, sweeping β ∈ { 0.5, 1, 2, 4, 8, 16 } produces certification rates and stopping times that are nearly unchanged relative to unweighted CITE. The e ff ective gap ratio ∆w/μ r is essentially the same as δ/ pr for β ≥ 2. This occurs because the top categories are already assigned weights close to one, so the logistic transformation does not substantially separate the mode from its runner-up. G.5. Rank-Based Weight Model. The rank-based model assigns E[W | X = a] = 0.95 · e−γ·rank( a)

for the top K0 = 10 categories and wlow = 0.1 otherwise, with additive Uniform (−0.05 , 0.05) noise clipped to [0 .01 , 1]. Table 9 reports detailed results for Setting 5 at N = 512. We omit a separate figure here to avoid duplicating Figure 3; the table directly shows how the e ff ective weighted gap and stopping time change with the rank-decay parameter. Table 9. Setting 5: rank-based W-CITE at N = 512 in the supplementary weight sweep.

In this synthetic rank-based model, performance improves as the e ff ective gap ratio ∆w/μ r

increases. A modest γ = 0.2 already reaches full certification in this sweep and reduces ¯τ by about 40%; at γ = 1.0, the reduction is about 80%. AIME 2026. All 30 problems of AIME 2026 (Dekoninck et al., 2026) (AIME I and AIME II combined), each with an integer gold answer in [0 , 999]. Prompt is the problem statement with a per-model su ffi x: “Solve in under 100 tokens. Put your final answer within \boxed{} .” (Qwen3-30B-A3B-Instruct-2507) or “Do not show your reasoning. Put only the final answer within \boxed{} .” (gpt-oss-20b). Answers are extracted from the last \boxed{...} and matched by exact integer equality. FrontierScience-Olympiad. FrontierScience-Olympiad (Wang et al., 2026) contains 100 olympiad-level physics, chemistry, and biology problems with numeric or symbolic answers. We use a 10-problem subset obtained by taking every 10th problem (problems 10, 20, . . . , 100). Prompt su ffi xis the same as for AIME 2026. Each extracted answer is compared against the gold by an LLM self-judge (the generating model decides whether its own output is equivalent to the gold). ER-REASON.. ER-REASON (Mehandru et al., 2025) contains 3,984 emergency-room encounters. We use the 72-encounter subset with expert-authored clinical rationales, which the paper designates as the primary evaluation set. Both models use a two-turn chat prompt in place of the \boxed{} 

55 su ffi x. Turn 1 asks the model to assign an ESI acuity level (1–5) from the chief complaint, demographics, and triage vital signs. Turn 2, which echoes the Turn 1 acuity, asks for the disposition (one of 12 labels) given the full ED provider note and all historical notes. The final answer is parsed as the fixed string “Triage: ⟨acuity ⟩ | Disposition: ⟨disposition ⟩”. H.3. Per-problem certification rates: Qwen3-30B-A3B-Instruct-2507.

Hardware and inference server. Real-LLM experiments are conducted using NVIDIA A100 80 GB GPUs with vLLM as the inference server. Data and code access. All code will be open-sourced upon publication.
